Keywords:
race, ethnicity, multiracial, diversity, law students
Abstract:
The high-stakes nature of law school testing and admissions puts a premium on the student data presented to admissions committees, such as essays, academic and work history, and student background characteristics including race/ethnicity. 4,472 law school-bound students self-identified their race/ethnicity using (a) a mutually exclusive "choose one" format during registration for the law school admissions test, and (b) an elaborated "check-all-that-apply" format as part of a national survey administered during the first weeks at their chosen law school. Student multiraciality that was masked by the first assessment was associated with self-reported ethnic identity, discrimination experience, intergroup contact, race-related attitudes, academic performance, and trait ratings, as compared to monoracial majority students. A different profile of findings was observed across these constructs when multiracial students were compared to monoracial majority students, to monoracial minority students, and within group. These correlates also predicted the likelihood of changing identification across the two assessment contexts. These findings support the continued study of specific combinations of multiracial groups, fluidity of multiracial identities, and context effects that influence race/ethnicity self-categorizations. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Microaggressions within families: Experiences of multiracial people.
Authors:
Nadal, Kevin L., John Jay College of Criminal Justice, City University of New York, New York, NY, US, knadal@jjay.cuny.edu
Sriken, Julie, John Jay College of Criminal Justice, City University of New York, New York, NY, US

Abstract:
This study illustrates the types of multiracial microaggressions, or subtle forms of discrimination toward multiracial people, that transpire in family settings. Utilizing a Consensual Qualitative Research (CQR) Method and a Qualitative Secondary Analysis (QSA), multiracial participants (N = 9) were interviewed in three focus groups to describe the types of microaggressions they encounter in their families. Five domains emerged including (a) isolation within the family, (b) favoritism within the family, (c) questioning of authenticity, (d) denial of multiracial identity and experiences by monoracial family members, and (e) feelings about not learning about family heritage or culture. We discuss how encouraging discussions of race and ethnicity in multiracial families is conducive to promoting healthier identities and well-being for multiracial people. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2013 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Abstract:
Multiracial individuals report that the social pressure of having to “choose” one of their racial groups is a primary source of psychological conflict. Yet because of their ability to maneuver among their multiple identities, multiracials also adopt flexible cognitive strategies in dealing with their social environments—demonstrating a benefit to having multiple racial identities. The current article reviews recent research involving multiracial participants to examine the behavioral and cognitive outcomes linked to being multiracial and pinpoints possible moderators that may affect these outcomes. Limitations in applying monoracial identity frameworks to multiracial populations are also discussed. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2015 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Monoracial and biracial children: Effects of racial identity saliency on social learning and social preferences.
Authors:
Gaither, Sarah E., Tufts University, Medford, MA, US, sarah.gaither@tufts.edu
Chen, Eva E., Hong Kong University of Science and Technology, Hong Kong

Language:

Keywords:
monoracial & biracial children, racial identity, social learning, social preferences
Abstract:
Children prefer learning from, and affiliating with, their racial in‐group but those preferences may vary for biracial children. Monoracial (White, Black, Asian) and biracial (Black/White, Asian/White) children (N = 246, 3–8 years) had their racial identity primed. In a learning preferences task, participants determined the function of a novel object after watching adults (White, Black, and Asian) demonstrate its uses. In the social preferences task, participants saw pairs of children (White, Black, and Asian) and chose with whom they most wanted to socially affiliate. Biracial children showed flexibility in racial identification during learning and social tasks. However, minority‐primed biracial children were not more likely than monoracial minorities to socially affiliate with primed racial in‐group members, indicating their in‐group preferences are contextually based. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2015 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Abstract:
Despite the recent growth in multiracial children among American children, we know very little about their well-being. Using the Early Childhood Longitudinal Study, Kindergarten Class (N = 17,706), we evaluated the likelihood of living in poverty and near poverty for multiracial and monoracial children. Most multiracial groups have poverty or near poverty rates that are in between Whites and their respective minority monoracial counterparts, with Asian-Whites reporting a risk of poverty equivalent to Whites and Black-Hispanics reporting risks as high as Black and Hispanic children. Family structure and educational and occupational attainment characteristics explain some or all of the White/non-White differentials in poverty for multiracial groups, even as monoracial children still exhibit higher risk than Whites. We consider these patterns’ implications for practitioners working with low-income families. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2013 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Multiracial faces: How categorization affects memory at the boundaries of race.
Authors:
Pauker, Kristin, Tufts University, Medford, MA, US, Kristin.Pauker@Tufts.edu
Ambady, Nalini, Tufts University, Medford, MA, US
Address:
Pauker, Kristin, Department of Psychology, Tufts University, 490 Boston Avenue, Medford, MA, US, 02155, Kristin.Pauker@Tufts.edu
Source:
Journal of Social Issues, Vol 65(1), Mar, 2009. pp. 69-86.
Page Count:
18
Publisher:
United Kingdom : Wiley-Blackwell Publishing Ltd.
Other Publishers:
United Kingdom : Blackwell Publishing
ISSN:
0022-4537 (Print)
1540-4560 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
multiracial faces, memory, race, ethnicity, categorization, face recognition, biracial adults
Abstract:
Monoracial and multiracial individuals are likely to have different conceptualizations of race and subsequently different approaches toward racial ambiguity. In particular, monoracial individuals may be more likely to rely on categories when processing ambiguous faces, whereas multiracial individuals may tend to ignore such categorizations due to a reduced tendency to essentialize race. We compared monoracial (White and Asian) and biracial (Asian/White) individuals’ memory patterns. Specifically, we examined participants’ memory for White, Asian, and biracial faces labelled as either White or Asian. Both White and Asian participants relied on the labels, remembering faces labeled as the in-group better than faces labeled as the out-group. Biracial participants relied less on the labels, exhibiting better recognition memory overall. Biracial participants’ memory performance was also highly correlated with a less essentialist view of human traits. This cognitive flexibility may serve an adaptive function for biracial individuals and contribute to enhanced facial recognition. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Natural ambiguities: Racial categorization of multiracial individuals.
Authors:
Chen, Jacqueline M., University of California, Santa Barbara, CA, US, j_chen@psych.ucsb.edu
Hamilton, David L., University of California, Santa Barbara, CA, US
Address:
Chen, Jacqueline M., Department of Psychological and Brain Sciences, University of California, Santa Barbara, CA, US, 93106-9660, j_chen@psych.ucsb.edu
Source:
Journal of Experimental Social Psychology, Vol 48(1), Jan, 2012. pp. 152-164.
Page Count:
13
Publisher:
Netherlands : Elsevier Science
ISSN:
0022-1031 (Print)
Language:
English
Abstract:
Understanding the perception of multiracial persons is increasingly important in today's diverse society. The present research investigated the process of categorizing multiracial persons as “Multiracial.” We hypothesized that perceivers would make fewer Multiracial categorizations of multiracials and that these categorizations would take longer than monoracial categorizations. We found support for these hypotheses across six experiments. Experiment 1 demonstrated that perceivers did not categorize morphed Black–White faces as Multiracial with the same frequency with which they categorized Black and White faces as Black and White (respectively), and that categorizations of multiracials as Multiracial took longer than monoracial categorizations. Experiment 2 replicated and extended these effects to real Black–White faces. Experiment 3 showed that these findings generalized to Asian–White faces. We used pixel variance analysis to show that these effects were not due to increased variance among Multiracial faces. The image analysis showed that the Black–White morphs and real biracials were actually less varied than either the Black or White sets of faces. Experiments 4 and 5 demonstrated that cognitive load and time constraints detrimentally affected multiracial, but not monoracial, categorizations. Experiment 6 showed that imbuing monoracial categories with importance decreases the use of the Multiracial category. Implications of these findings for understanding perceptions of multiracial persons are discussed. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Consumer culture theory. Belk, Russell W. (Ed); Price, Linda (Ed); Peñaloza, Lisa (Ed); pp. 41-56; Bingley, United Kingdom: Emerald Group Publishing; Vol 15; 2013. xvii, 389 pp.

0885-2111 (Print)

Language:

Keywords:
multiracial identity, consumer behavior, marketing, phenomenological approach
Abstract:
Purpose: The purpose of this chapter is to explore the intersection of identity, culture, and consumption as it relates to multiracial identity development. Methodology/approach: The authors employed a phenomenological approach wherein 21 multiracial women were interviewed to understanding the lived experience and meaning of multiracial identity development. Findings: Findings of this study indicate that multiracial consumers engage with the marketplace to assuage racial discordance and legitimize the liminal space they occupy. Research implications: While there is much research related to the variety of ways marketing and consumption practices intersect with identity (re)formation, researchers have focused much of their attention on monoracial populations. This research identifies and fills a gap in the literature related to how multiple racial backgrounds complicate this understanding. Practical implications: Due to their growing social visibility and recognized buying power, multiracial individuals have emerged as a viable consumer segment among marketers. However, there is a dearth of research examining how multiracial populations experience the marketplace. Originality/value: This study provides a better understanding of the ways in which multiracial individuals utilize consumption practices as a means of developing and expressing their racial identity. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2014 APA, all rights reserved) (chapter)

Keywords:

Abstract:
Mixed-race or multiethnic youth are at risk for mental and physical health problems. We used data from the National Longitudinal Study of Youth 1997 to compare family characteristics of adolescents of a mixed-race or multiethnic background with those of a monoracial or monoethnic background. Mixed-race or multiethnic youth reported feeling less supported by parents and reported less satisfactory parent-adolescent relationships. Mixed-race/multiethnic youth were more like monoracial White youth in terms of being independent but were more like racial or ethnic minorities (African Americans, Hispanics) in regard to family activities. Reasons for these findings are explored. We discuss the need for future research on the experiences of mixed-race/multiethnic youth. Title:
Passing as black: Racial identity work among biracial Americans.
Authors:
Khanna, Nikki, University of Vermont, Burlington, VT, US, nkhanna@uvm.edu
Johnson, Cathryn, Emory University, Atlanta, GA, US
Address:
Khanna, Nikki, Department of Sociology, University of Vermont, 31 South Prospect Street, Burlington, VT, US, 05452, nkhanna@uvm.edu
Source:
Social Psychology Quarterly, Vol 73(4), Dec, 2010. pp. 380-397.
Page Count:
18
Publisher:
US : Sage Publications
Other Journal Titles:

Other Publishers:
US : American Sociological Assn
US : American Sociological Society
US : Beacon House
ISSN:
0190-2725 (Print)
1939-8999 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
racial identity work, biracial Americans, motivation
Abstract:
Drawing on interview data with black-white biracial adults, we examine the considerable agency most have in asserting their racial identities to others. Extending research on "identity work" (Snow and Anderson 1987), we explore the strategies biracial people use to conceal (i.e., pass), cover, and/or accent aspects of their racial ancestries, and the individual and structural-level factors that limit the accessibility and/or effectiveness of some strategies. We further find that how these biracial respondents identify is often contextual—most identify as biracial, but in some contexts, they pass as monoracial. Scholars argue that passing may be a relic of the past, yet we find that passing still occurs today. Most notably, we find a striking reverse pattern of passing today—while passing during the Jim Crow era involved passing as white, these respondents more often report passing as black today. Motivations for identity work are explored, with an emphasis on passing as black. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2013 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Perceptions and experiences in higher education: A national study of multiracial Asian American and Latino/a students in psychology.
Authors:
Smith, Tara D., Department of Psychology, University of Maryland-Baltimore County, Baltimore, MD, US, tarasingh@hotmail.com
Maton, Kenneth I., Department of Psychology, University of Maryland-Baltimore County, Baltimore, MD, US

Keywords:
multiracial, barriers, supports, higher education
Abstract:
Demographic trends suggest increasing numbers of multiple racial heritage students attending U.S. campuses and universities, a change reflected within psychology. However, there is little empirical investigation into the educational experiences and needs of multiracials. The current study (the second in a series of studies to use data from a national survey of psychology graduate and undergraduate students) compared 2 multiracial groups, Asian American/European American and Latino/a/European Americans, with their single-heritage counterparts on several variables of interest—academic supports and barriers, linkage between barriers faced and ethnicity, and perceived cultural diversity. Results indicated that multiracial groups reported more of a link between academic barriers experienced and their ethnicity than European American students, but less of a link than their monoracial minority peers. No differences between groups were found related to academic supports, academic barriers, and perceived cultural diversity. Study limitations, future research, and implications are discussed. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2015 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Personality characteristic adaptations: Multiracial adolescents' patterns of racial self-identification change.
Authors:
Terry, Rodney L., Howard University, Washington, DC, US, rodney.terry@census.gov
Winston, Cynthia E., Howard University, Washington, DC, US
Address:
Terry, Rodney L., U.S. Census Bureau, Statistical Research Division, Washington, DC, US, 20233, rodney.terry@census.gov
Source:
Journal of Research on Adolescence, Vol 20(2), Jun, 2010. pp. 432-455.
Page Count:

United Kingdom : Wiley-Blackwell Publishing Ltd.
Other Publishers:
United Kingdom : Blackwell Publishing
ISSN:
1050-8392 (Print)
1532-7795 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
personality characteristic adaptations, multiracial adolescents, racial self-identification change
Abstract:
For multiracial adolescents, forming a sense of self and identity can be complicated, even at the level of classifying themselves in terms of racial group membership. Using a Race Self Complexity (Winston et al., 2004) theoretical framework, this study used an open-ended question to examine the racial self-identification fluidity of 66 adolescents during the 7th, 8th, and 11th grades. This sample included 22 Black/White multiracial adolescents, as well as a matched sample of 22 Black and 22 White adolescents. Seventy-three percent of the multiracial adolescents changed their racial self-identification in the form of two time change patterns with a number of consolidating and differentiating racial self-identification variations. There was no change for the monoracial adolescents. These results suggest that within the lives of multiracial adolescents, the process of racial self-identification may be a personality characteristic adaptation to the meaning of race in American society that may change across time, place, and role. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Perspectives and Research on the Positive and Negative Implications of Having Multiple Racial Identities.
Authors:
Shih, Margaret, Department of Psychology, University of Michigan, Ann Arbor, MI, US, mjshih@umich.edu
Sanchez, Diana T., Department of Psychology, Rutgers, The State University of New Jersey, NJ, US
Address:
Shih, Margaret, Department of Psychology, University of Michigan, 525 East University, Ann Arbor, MI, US, 48109, mjshih@umich.edu

Page Count:

US : American Psychological Association
Other Publishers:
US : Psychological Review Company
US : The Macmillan Company
US : The Review Publishing Company

0033-2909 (Print)
1939-1455 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
multiracial identity, biracial identity, race, identity development, multiple identities, depression, problem behaviors, relationships, self-esteem, school performance, adjustment
Abstract:
Much attention has been directed toward understanding the impact having a multiracial background has on psychological well-being and adjustment. Past psychological research has focused on the challenges multiracial individuals confront in defining a racial identity. The implication is that these challenges lead to outcomes that are psychologically detrimental. However, evidence to support this assertion is mixed. The authors review qualitative and quantitative empirical research examining multiracial individuals' identity development, depression, problem behaviors, peer relationships, school performance, and self-esteem, finding support for detrimental outcomes only in studies sampling clinical populations. Studies on nonclinical samples find that multiracial individuals tend to be just as well-adjusted as their monoracial peers on most psychological outcomes. Earlier assertions of maladjustment may have been due to reliance on qualitative research that sampled clinical populations. Other implications and future research are discussed. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Politics and policies: Attitudes toward multiracial Americans.
Authors:
Campbell, Mary E., Department of Sociology, University of Iowa, Iowa City, IA, US, mary-e-campbell@uiowa.edu
Herman, Melissa R., Department of Sociology, Dartmouth College, Hanover, NH, US, melissa.r.herman@dartmouth.edu
Address:
Campbell, Mary E., Department of Sociology, University of Iowa, 140 Seashore Hall West, Iowa City, IA, US, 52242, mary-e-campbell@uiowa.edu
Source:

Page Count:

Publisher:
United Kingdom : Taylor & Francis
ISSN:
0141-9870 (Print)
1466-4356 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
politics, anti-discrimination policies, attitudes, multiracial Americans
Abstract:
The growing prominence of the multiracial population in the United States is prompting new questions about attitudes toward multiracial people and popular opinion of policies designed to protect them from discrimination. Currently, American anti-discrimination policies are directed at groups who identify with a single race, but the rising profile of multiracial groups introduces new complexity into questions about racial policy. In this study, we find generally positive affect toward multiracial people, although monoracial minorities are more positive toward multiracial people than whites are. About half of the monoracial minorities and the majority of whites oppose including multiracial people in anti-discrimination policies. Attitudes are associated with traditional predictors such as education and political beliefs, and also with the racial heterogeneity of the local context and intimate contact with other racial groups. Although multiracial people report experiencing discrimination at levels similar to those of monoracial minorities, our results suggest there may be significant resistance to anti-discrimination policies that include multiracial groups. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Positioning multiraciality in cyberspace: Treatment of multiracial daters in an online dating website.
Authors:
Curington, Celeste Vaughan, Sociology Department, University of Massachusetts-Amherst, Amherst, MA, US, ccuringt@soc.umass.edu
Lin, Ken-Hou, Population Research Center, University of Texas at Austin, Austin, TX, US
Lundquist, Jennifer Hickes, Sociology Department, University of Massachusetts Amherst, Amherst, MA, US
Address:
Curington, Celeste Vaughan, Department of Sociology, University of Massachusetts-Amherst, 630 Thompson Hall, Amherst, MA, US, 01003, ccuringt@soc.umass.edu
Source:
American Sociological Review, Vol 80(4), Aug, 2015. pp. 764-788.
Page Count:
25
Publisher:
US : Sage Publications
Other Publishers:
US : American Sociological Assn
ISSN:
0003-1224 (Print)
1939-8271 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
racial identity, multiracial, assortive mating, online dating
Abstract:
The U.S. multiracial population has grown substantially in the past decades, yet little is known about how these individuals are positioned in the racial hierarchies of the dating market. Using data from one of the largest dating websites in the United States, we examine how monoracial daters respond to initial messages sent by multiracial daters with various White/non-White racial and ethnic makeups. We test four different theories: hypodescent, multiracial in-betweenness, White equivalence, and what we call a multiracial dividend effect. We find no evidence for the operation of hypodescent. Asian-White daters, in particular, are afforded a heightened status, and Black-White multiracials are treated as an in-between group. For a few specific multiracial gender groups, we find evidence for a dividend effect, where multiracial men and women are preferred above all other groups, including Whites. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2015 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Posttraumatic cognitions, somatization, and PTSD severity among Asian American and White college women with sexual trauma histories.
Authors:
Koo, Kelly H., Department of Psychology, University of Washington, Seattle, WA, US, kelly.koo@va.gov
Nguyen, Hong V., Department of Psychology, University of Washington, Seattle, WA, US
Gilmore, Amanda K., Department of Psychology, University of Washington, Seattle, WA, US

Address:

Source:
Psychological Trauma: Theory, Research, Practice, and Policy, Vol 6(4), Jul, 2014. pp. 337-344.
Page Count:
8
Publisher:
US : Educational Publishing Foundation
ISSN:
1942-9681 (Print)
1942-969X (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
Asian Americans, PTSD, posttraumatic cognitions, race, somatization, White Americans, sexual trauma histories
Abstract:
The need for trauma research with monoracial groups such as Asian Americans (AAs) has recently been emphasized to better understand trauma experiences and inform interventions across populations. Given AA cultural contexts, posttraumatic cognitions and somatization may be key in understanding trauma experiences for this group. AA and White American (WA) trauma-exposed college women completed a survey on sexual trauma history, posttraumatic cognitions, somatic symptoms, and PTSD severity. For the overall sample, higher negative cognitions were associated with higher somatization. Asian race was associated with higher negative cognitions, which then predicted higher PTSD. Unexpectedly, WAs more strongly endorsed somatization than AAs. These findings indicate that posttraumatic cognitions may be helpful in understanding relationships between somatization and PTSD severity among those of Asian backgrounds and that the relationship between somatization and PTSD symptoms is culturally complex. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2015 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Poverty at a racial crossroads: Poverty among multiracial children of single mothers.
Authors:
Bratter, Jenifer L., Rice University, Houston, TX, US, jbratter@rice.edu
Damaske, Sarah, Pennsylvania State University, University Park, PA, US
Address:
Bratter, Jenifer L., Department of Sociology, Rice University, MS-28, 6100 Main Street, Houston, TX, US, 77005, jbratter@rice.edu
Source:
Journal of Marriage and Family, Vol 75(2), Apr, 2013. pp. 486-502.
Page Count:
17
Publisher:
United Kingdom : Wiley-Blackwell Publishing Ltd.
Other Journal Titles:
Journal of Marriage and the Family; Living; Marriage and Family Living
Other Publishers:
US : National Council on Family Relations
ISSN:
0022-2445 (Print)
1741-3737 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
poverty, multiracial children, single mothers
Abstract:
Although multiracial youth represent a growing segment of children in all American families, we have little information on their well-being within single-mother households. This article examines multiracial children’s level of poverty within single-mother families to identify the degree to which they may stand out from their monoracial peers. Using data from the 2006–2008 American Community Survey (3-year estimates), we explore the level of racial disparities in child poverty between monoracial White children and monoracial and multiracial children of color. Fully adjusted multivariate logistic regression analyses (n = 359,588) reveal that nearly all children of color are more likely to be poor than White children. Yet many multiracial children appear to hold an in-between status in which they experience lower rates of poverty than monoracial children of color. The high level of variation across groups suggests that the relationship between race and childhood poverty is more complicated than generally presumed. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2013 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Abstract:
A large number of adolescents of interracial ancestry (parents comprising various combinations of African-American, American Indian/Alaska Native, European-American, Chinese, Filipino, Hispanic, Japanese, Korean, Puerto Rican, Samoan, and Tongan ancestry) were contrasted with a monoracial European-American sample in the degree to which they reported symptoms of depression, anxiety, conduct disorder/aggression, and substance abuse. Title:

Keywords:

Abstract:
Racial attitudes of 60 preschool children (aged 3–6 yrs; 28 boys, 32 girls) from either a monoracial Euro-American child-care program (n = 16), a monoracial African-American program (n = 12), or a multiracial program (25 Euro-Americans, 7 African-Americans) were assessed using the Preschool Racial Attitudes Measure II. Despite the overall neutral attitudes reflected by these children, evidence of a Euro-American bias among older children (aged >4.5 yrs) was found. Replication with a large, randomly selected sample is desirable. Recognizing and understanding early racial attitudes may be a key factor in fostering positive racial identity and preventing the formation of prejudice. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved)

Title:
Racial identification among multiracial youth: Implications for adjustment.
Authors:
Herman, Melissa R., Dartmouth College, Hanover, NH, US
Source:
Handbook of race, racism, and the developing child. Quintana, Stephen M. (Ed); McKown, Clark (Ed); pp. 203-225; Hoboken, NJ, US: John Wiley & Sons Inc; 2008. xii, 507 pp.
Page Count:
23
ISBN:
978-0-470-04322-6 (Hardcover)
Language:
English
Keywords:
multiracial identity development, monoracial youth, multiracial youth, racial identification
Abstract:
In this chapter I cover both the determinants of racial identification among multiracial youth and its implications for their mental health, achievement, and self-esteem. The goals for this chapter are, first, to describe the literature on identity development among biracials as a way of challenging the notion of race as a single and fixed aspect of identity. This first goal leads logically to the second goal, which is to examine the developmental implications of flexible racial identity. Understanding these implications for multiracial youth may even shed some light on the racial identity development of monoracial youth. In keeping with my two goals, I organize the literature into two sections: one on identity development, and the other on the impact of identity on child and adolescent outcomes. Within the section on identity development, I include three subsections. The first describes general theories of multiracial identity development, the second provides details about specific race combinations (part-Black, part-Asian, part-Hispanic, part-American Indian), and the third explores the connections between context and racial identification. In the section on the impact of identity on developmental outcomes, I discuss the distinction between ancestry and racial identity for multiracial youth and explore the ways that context mediates the identification- development link among multiracial youth. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (create)

Title:
Racial preference and biculturality in biracial preschoolers.
Authors:
Johnson, Deborah J., U Wisconsin, Madison, US
Source:
Merrill-Palmer Quarterly, Vol 38(2), Apr, 1992. pp. 233-244.
Page Count:

Language:
English
Keywords:

Abstract:
Racial preferences of 16 biracial children were compared with the preferences of 28 Black and 18 White children. Findings from the Preschool Racial Attitude Measure II (PRAM II) indicate that mean preferences of biracial Ss were not significantly different from either of their monoracial cohorts. However, mean preferences of Black and White Ss were significantly different, such that White Ss were more pro-White than Black Ss. PRAM II scores for biracial Ss were primarily based on the combination of positive affect toward White and negative affect toward Black. Although Black Ss made negative choices toward Black, their racial attitude score was dependent on the balance of positive choices for Black and White. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved)

Title:
Racial preferences and identifications of White elementary schoolchildren.
Authors:
Friedman, Philip, Howard U
Source:
Contemporary Educational Psychology, Vol 5(3), Jul, 1980. pp. 256-265.
Page Count:

Netherlands : Elsevier Science

Language:

Keywords:

Abstract:
Examined the racial preferences and identifications of White children in both a White monoracial and a multiracial setting. A doll selection procedure originally described by K. B. Clark and M. K. Clark (1939, 1947) to study racial awareness and preferences in Black children was employed with 120 kindergarten, 1st-, and 3rd-grade children. Most Ss showed a marked preference for White over comparable Black dolls. However, more Black doll selections were made in multiracial than in monoracial schools, particularly among male Ss. White doll color preference also significantly decreased at the 3rd-grade level. This finding was consistently observed in response to each racial preference question. Neither race of the interviewer nor sex of the child had a significant effect on doll color preferences. Few errors were made in response to a series of 5 racial identification questions. (27 ref) (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved)

School racial composition and biracial adolescents' school attachment.

Klugman, Joshua, Temple University, Philadelphia, PA, US

Abstract:
Despite extensive research on multiracial youth in recent years, to date, no empirical studies have analyzed how racial context may affect biracial adolescents' sense of belonging in a social institution beyond families. In this study, we examine how the racial makeup of the student body affects self-identified biracial adolescents' school attachment. Using data from the National Longitudinal Study of Adolescent Health, we find that the proportions of white or black students in school significantly affect the school attachment of Hispanic/black, Asian/black, and American Indian/black biracial adolescents, but school racial composition in general has little influence on biracial adolescents with a partial-white identification (i.e., black/white, Hispanic/white, Asian/white, and American Indian/white). Our analyses also show that on average, students of most biracial groups display lower school attachment than their corresponding monoracial groups, but the differences from the monoracial groups with the lower school attachment are generally small. We discuss the implications of our findings for biracial adolescents' perceived racial boundaries and contemporary American race relations. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

PsycINFO

Small Samples, Big Challenges: Studying Atypical Family Forms 

We discuss the challenges of small-subsample sizes that family scholars often encounter when studying nontraditional or less common family types. We begin by identifying the general difficulties of using existing data in this line of research and then discuss potential solutions that may help researchers to avoid these problems. Using data from the Early Childhood Longitudinal Study, we use the example of monoracial White, monoracial Asian, and Asian/White interracial families to illustrate these problems and available, albeit imperfect, solutions.

Key Words: gay and lesbian families; interracial families; small samples.; atypical families

Social scientists have witnessed a transformation in the definition of "family," which now comprises, among others, traditional two-parent households, single-parent and stepparent homes, and cohabiting, gay and lesbian, and interracial couples. This development has been accompanied by research using nationally representative data to complement the rich qualitative studies and smaller scale surveys on such topics as childrearing practices and parent-child interactions among these families (Albrecht, Miller, & Clarke, 1994; Kurdek, 1994; Lee & Bean, 2004). Unfortunately, research efforts in this direction are often discounted for their reliance on small sizes of atypical families in their samples, leading to two potential problems: (a) sensitivity to misidentified observations and incomplete data information and (b) underestimation of real effects.

In this paper, we discuss the challenges resulting from small-subsample sizes that family scholars often encounter when using existing survey data to study nontraditional or less common families. To illustrate the fragility of empirical findings derived from small subsamples or comparisons of dramatically unequal-size groups, especially when few or no statistically significant differences occur, we use data from the Early Childhood Longitudinal Study, Kindergarten Class of 1998-1999 (ECLS-K) to examine the school performance of children from monoracial White, monoracial Asian, and Asian/White interracial families. We also discuss the utility of power analysis in determining appropriate subsample sizes and solutions that scholars from other fields have proposed to partially reconcile these problems.

THE CHALLENGE

Although nationally representative data often oversample certain groups (e.g., African Americans), nontraditional families often are neither oversampled nor even considered in these data collection efforts. As a result, family scholars often face two challenges, as discussed below.

Misidentified Observations and Incomplete Data Information

Existing data sources typically do not include questions that allow a straightforward identification of certain family types, such as gay and lesbian families. Although researchers can sometimes develop innovative strategies to locate the target samples of their interest, questions arise as to whether the definition and operationalization of their empirical constructs yield reasonable reliability and validity. This problem is worsened if respondents provide incomplete information during the survey or misunderstand questions. For example, Powell's research team recently completed a series of interviews on people's views regarding families. At least 2% of the respondents, but upward to 5%, did not understand the meaning of such words as heterosexual, sibling, or partner. Although interviews can be problematic, especially if interviewers are poorly trained and do not sufficiently probe inconsistencies or nonresponses, surveys may be even more so, given variations in reading ability and simple errors in marking responses. As an illustration, directors of the National Longitudinal Study of Adolescent Health recently detected errors in the measurement of parental gender, a problem that produced an overestimate of same-gender parents.

In the absence of large subsamples of atypical families, the potentially detrimental consequences of incomplete data and misidentified observations can be considerable. When the missing data or misidentifications are random and infrequent, the resulting estimates are inefficient at minimum and may be biased if the errors are in the key independent variables in the analysis. When the errors are nonrandom, the coefficients for the independent variable of interest, as well as other explanatory variables, are almost always biased. To rigorously assess the influences of missing data and misidentified observations, researchers may assign prespecified measurement errors to the covariates of interest and then perform simulation experiments. In most cases, however, it is difficult to predict whether the errors lead to upward or downward biases without knowing the exact patterns of these measurement errors in each individual study.

To address the problem of incomplete data information, social scientists have long used ad hoc methods, such as listwise deletion and mean substitution. Applied statisticians suggest that multiple imputation allows a more efficient use of existing data and thus is a better solution for the missing data problem (Acock, 2005, this issue; Allison, 2002). Although multiple imputation is a simulation-based technique relying on large-sample approximations, simulation studies have shown that the method can be used with data sets as small as N = 50 (Graham & Schafer, 1999). Because the results of multiple imputation tend to accurately reflect uncertainty resulting from missing data and ordinary sampling variability, it should be used in small subsamples to prevent further decreases in the subgroup sizes in the data.

Misidentification of atypical families is a more difficult problem to solve. To a certain extent, researchers may rely on the extant literature as a source to check whether the subsample identified from the data appropriately represents the atypical families. For example, if past studies using qualitative data, small-scale surveys, or the census provided a general sociodemographic profile of an emergent family type, one can examine whether the selected subsample presents seemingly idiosyncratic patterns in these characteristics. In particular, if previous studies have examined the atypical families of interest using similar analytical strategies, Bayesian methods can be used to combine such prior information into the current study and to adjust for misclassification errors (see Gustafson, 2004, and Hahn and Raghunathan, 1988, for detailed discussions). This approach is viable, however, only if reliable and quantifiable information on the family types is available.

Another strategy to minimize the likelihood of misidentification is to categorize the observations using at least one alternative measurement strategy. When the empirical constructs are reliable measures, this triangulation leads to the same group of observations and thus increases the variables' construct validity. This strategy may also lead to an alternative scenario, however, in which the researchers may find an insufficient number or inconsistent set of observations. When this occurs, the researchers need to reconcile the inconsistencies, seek a different data source, or discontinue the study. Several years ago, for example, we explored the possibility of studying children from gay and lesbian households using the National Educational Longitudinal Study. Although the use of one measure resulted in a reasonably large number of same-gender households, we concluded that the profound disjuncture between this measure and other measures in these data presented an insurmountable problem and thus effectively precluded any meaningful examination of gay and lesbian families.

Small-Subsample Sizes

Even if one is confident that a particular family type is accurately designated in a data set, the resulting small-subsample size may underestimate its real effect. Nock (2001) recently critiqued the extant literature on gay and lesbian families for, among other reasons, its reliance on small subsamples, which increases the risk of overlooking the differences among various family structures. He argued for a minimum sample of 800 gay and lesbian parents in analyses comparing gay and lesbian families and other family types. Although this figure seems unnecessarily large and almost impossible to obtain given current constraints on research on same-gender families, we agree that the subsample sizes in some studies are insufficiently large to reach a minimum level of statistical power, an issue we discuss in greater detail later.

Even if two family types actually differ, comparisons using small subsamples may yield only marginally significant differences (e.g., p = .10). Although the standard advice is researchers using small samples should accept larger p values as evidence against the null hypothesis, we caution that even a more liberal standard of significance may overlook meaningful differences.

Underestimation is a more serious problem when even marginal statistical significance is not found. Because the literature often suggests that atypical and traditional families may exert different influences on their children, it may be appealing to conclude that a finding of "no findings" means that the atypical families in question may not be as disadvantaged or advantaged as is commonly assumed. Others may reasonably counter, however, that the small-subsample size obscures differences between the focal atypical families and their counterparts.

Over the past few years, applied statisticians have made considerable progress in the analysis of small samples (Hoyle, 1999). Although their suggestions usually are aimed specifically at experimental research designs, their insights are readily applicable to analysis using secondary data sets. In general, these recommendations converge in two major directions: (a) reducing error variance in the dependent measures and (b) increasing statistical power by combining the effect sizes of analyses using similar outcome measures, research designs, and intervening variables. Because the use of multiple outcome measures enhances the possibility of reducing measurement errors and combining effect sizes, we recommend that researchers examine multiple dependent variables under the same conceptual dimension instead of using one dependent measure (see Powell and Downey, 1997, for an illustration of this approach). Doing so allows the researchers to search for a consistent pattern of differences (or nondifferences) between atypical families and other family structures and to increase the statistical power of their analyses. Note that the actual statistical power of this design can vary by the effect sizes and other methodological features of specific studies. Nevertheless, the general utility and rationale of using multiple measures should be obvious. Similar solutions have been proposed for studies using other research designs. These are discussed in the last section of this paper.

AN ILLUSTRATION: ASIAN/WHITE INTERRACIAL FAMILIES Assessing the Implications of Small-Sample Sizes

The use of multiple dependent measures does not entirely solve the small-subsample size problem. To further assess the implications of a small subsample in a given study that compares a small group of atypical families with their counterparts, it may be informative to ask two questions. First, suppose we have three family groups, A, B, and C, with A being the largest and the reference group (e.g., monoracial White families), B being a smaller group (monoracial Asian), and C being the smallest group (e.g., Asian/White interracial). Suppose also that quantitative analyses using these subsamples indicate that A significantly differs from B but not C. If we reduce B to the size of C, would the difference between B and A become statistically insignificant, as in the case of C versus A? This question can be answered by drawing a random sample of B with the new observation number n'B = nC and then performing the regression analysis as the models using the full sample.

Second, if we increase the size of C to B, would the comparison of C and A change from statistical insignificance to significance? To answer this question, we apply the concept of propensity score, which has been used in other educational and family studies (Morgan, 2001). Conceptually, this technique allows us to identify the "ideal type" atypical families on the basis of their preexisting characteristics. Using the multinomial logit model, we can calculate the conditional probabilities of different family types on the basis of their characteristics reported in the literature:

Multiple line equation(s) cannot be represented in ASCII text

where m is the family type, ranged from 1 to 3. X measures the background characteristics of atypical families suggested by the literature.

To replicate the random process of social science settings, we assign family types to each case in the sample by generating a uniform random number on the interval from 0 to 1. If this number is less than the value of Pr(y = 1), then the family type is A. If the random number is between Pr(y = 1) and Pr(y = 1) 1 Pr(y = 2), then the family type is B. Otherwise, the family type is C. Finally, we randomly select the required number of observations from Family Type C, add the observations to the sample, and then perform the regression analysis as in the full-sample model. Note that if nB and nC are drastically different, these procedures may have to be repeated more than once. We discuss our reservations concerning this last step and other related issues in greater detail in the illustration below.

Comparing Academic Performance of Asian/White and White Monoracial Families

Our empirical illustration explores whether children from Asian/White families, one of the most rapidly growing family groups in the United States (Xie & Goyette, 1997), differ academically from their school peers in White monoracial families (see Cheng and Powell, 2002, for a more detailed discussion of the state of the literature on experiences of multiracial families). Because Asian-female/White-male and White-female/Asian-male couples differ in their sociodemographic profiles (Qian, 1997), we also compare the educational performance of children from these two interracial combinations.

We draw data from the ECLS-K, a nationally representative data set collected by the National Center for Educational Statistics. After crosstabulating the racial backgrounds of the two parents in the households, we identify 8,211 monoracial White, 773 monoracial Asian, 102 White-father/Asian-mother, and 72 Asian-father/White-mother interracial families. Despite the small subsamples of Asian/White interracial families in ECLS-K, the characteristics of these subsamples are consistent with those reported in previous studies using the census data (Qian, 1997). Tables are available upon request.

Children's academic performance can be measured by multiple indicators. But because our goal here is to illustrate the implications of different-sized subsamples, we restrict our analysis to children's test scores in three distinct subject areas: mathematics, reading, and general knowledge. All scores are taken from standardized tests, with a mean of 50 and a standard deviation of 10. A more extensive analysis, however, could follow our earlier recommendation to incorporate even more indicators of academic performance and explore the degree to which whatever patterns found are robust. To simplify our example, we focus on Asian/White families differences from White families but not from Asian families. Table 1 presents the regression coefficients of full-sample analysis.

As shown in Table 1, before controls for sociodemographic factors are added, children from White-father/Asian-mother families have significantly higher math and reading scores than children from White monoracial families (p < .01). Although children from Asian-father/White-mother households also earn higher math and reading scores than their peers from White monoracial families, the difference in math standardized tests is not significant at the .05 level (p = .08). In addition, children of White-father/Asian-mother and Asian-father/White-mother families, respectively, have lower and higher scores on the general knowledge exams than their White monoracial counterparts, but these differences are not significant at the .05 level. Compared to White monoracial students, young children from Asian monoracial families have higher scores in math and reading standardized tests (p < .05) but lower scores in general knowledge (p < .01).

The patterns for children from Asian monoracial and White-father/Asian-mother families remain after taking into account the effects of family sociodemographic factors. In contrast, none of the test score differences between children from Asian-father/White-mother families and monoracial White children remain significant upon controls for family characteristics and other variables.

From these models, it may be tempting to conclude that the Asian/White advantage exists for White-father/Asian-mother families only. Closer inspection of the table, however, suggests an alternative interpretation: The insignificant effects of Asian-father/White-mother families may result from its smaller sample size (72 children in contrast to the 102 children from White-father/Asian-mother homes). To test this possibility, we randomly selected 72 of the 102 children from White-father/Asian-mother families in order to reduce its sample size to that of Asian-father/White-mother households. We then repeat the same regression as in Table 1. These results are displayed in Panel A of Table 2. This sample reduction does not markedly alter the significance of most items, with one notable exception: The difference in math performance between children from White-father/Asian-mother and White monoracial families is no longer significant at the .05 level. Because the significance level decreases to .07, if we were to use an alpha level of .05, we would conclude that the only difference between children from Asian/White and White monoracial families is in the reading scores for children from White-father/Asian-mother households.

Of course, it is possible that a different random selection of the 72 of the 102 cases might yield a different conclusion. Consequently, we used simulation analysis to run regressions of 500 different random samples of White-father/Asian-mother households. These analyses suggest that, for math achievements, a reduction of the subsample size from 102 to 72 leads to statistical insignificance at the .05 level in 235 (47%) of 500 regression analyses. If we use an alpha level of .10, then 120 (24%) of 500 regression analyses are not statistically significant.

To further demonstrate how the change in subsample sizes may alter our substantive conclusions, we again use children from Whitefather/Asian-mother families as the baseline model. Applying the technique introduced in the last section, we generate 30 cases of Whitefather/Asian-mother families, add them to the randomly selected subsample in Panel A, and then perform the same regression analysis. The results are presented in Panel B of Table 2. In contrast to Panel A but consistent with Table 1, children in White-father/Asian-mother families now significantly differ in math scores from children in White monoracial families (p = .02).

It is worth noting that the analysis presented in Panel B is likely to be a conservative check of the small-subsample problem. In the multinomial logit equation, we use the variables suggested by the literature to locate households with similar characteristics to White-father/Asian-mother families. In cases in which the subsample occupies a large proportion of the full sample, this may increase the likelihood of using the same observations twice in the new analysis, leading to smaller standard errors and p values. In the case of atypical families, however, this is usually not a problem because the majority of the identified observations (despite having similar background characteristics) are drawn from the nonatypical subsample. Because the math scores of the added observations lean toward the scores of the full sample, the new subsample of 102 White-father/Asianmother families is less likely to show significant differences than the subsample used in Table 1. Simulation analysis, again using 500 simulations, reveals that on average 2.3% (or 1.66 families) of the original subsample is selected into the implemented observations. Although this proportion is substantially higher than would be expected at random (.79% of the full sample), the problem of deflated standard errors is minimal. Supplementary analyses also suggest that, if we use the model for math scores in Panel A as the baseline, the likelihood of finding a p value smaller than .05 is 32.4% (162 of 500 simulations). This further confirms that the proposed check is a conservative one.

Table 3 summarizes the effects of changing subsample sizes on the achievement scores of children from the other two racial minority groups: Asian monoracial and Asian-father/White-mother homes (more detailed information on these models is available upon request). In the models using the full ECLS-K sample (top panel, first row), children from Asian monoracial households (n = 773) significantly differ at least at the .05 level from their counterparts in White monoracial families on mathematics, reading, and general knowledge exam scores, regardless of whether controls are included. But when we reduce the sample size of this subgroup to 102 (i.e., the size of Whitefather/Asian-mother families), we see that two coefficients change from statistical significance to insignificance and two coefficients decrease their significant levels from .01 to .05. Upon a further decrease of the subsample size to 72 (i.e., the size of Asian-father/White-mother families), we see that three of the six models become insignificant at the .10 level and one coefficient is significant only at the .10 level. In other words, conclusions regarding the difference between children from Asian and White monoracial families are sensitive to sample size.

In the lower panel of Table 3, we directly examine the influence of subsample size increases on children from Asian-father/White-mother families. When the number of these families is increased from 72 to 102, the model for math achievements (no controls) becomes significant at the .05 level, whereas the model for general knowledge scores (with controls) changes from insignificant to significant at the .10 level. If the subsample size increases from 102 to 773 (i.e., to equal the size of Asian monoracial families), the significance levels of the two models further increases. The results from this last analysis (lower panel, last row) are problematic, however. In data that contain drastically unequal subsample sizes, matching the sample size of the small subgroup to that of the large group can completely alter the properties of the original subsample. Regardless of how appealing the results are, they should not be used.

Statistical Power

This exercise demonstrates how findings are sensitive to subsample sizes, but it does not tell us how large a subsample should be to give us enough confidence that a nonsignificant difference really means that comparison groups do not differ. This is an issue of statistical power, or "the probability that will correctly lead to rejection of a false null hypothesis" (Greene, 2003, p. 126). In bivariate analysis, some statistical packages (e.g., Stata) allow a straightforward calculation of power using the formula (Rosner, 2000)

Multiple line equation(s) cannot be represented in ASCII text

where μ2 and μ2 are the means of Samples 1 and 2, σ1 and σ2 are the standard deviations, and n1 and n2 are the respective sample sizes. The r is the ratio of n2 to n1, α is the specified significant level, and 1 — β is the power. Using this formula, we can calculate that the bivariate analysis comparing the math scores of children from Asian-father/White-mother families yields a power of .374 or the probability of Type II error = (1 — .374) = .626. That is, given our sample size, in 63% of a hundred times, we would fail to reject the null hypothesis when the null is actually false. Alternatively, we can calculate the sample sizes that are required to have a power of .70, a minimum level in most of power analyses. Our estimate is that we would need 166 Asian-father/White-mother families (and because mean differences and relative proportions of this group to other families should remain the same as in the original sample, 19,842 non-Asian-father/White-mother families) to reach this minimum level.

The above calculations are likely to be conservative estimates in multiple regression analysis. In multivariate analyses, power typically decreases as more variables are used in the equation. Kraemer and Thiemann (1987, pp. 105-112) provide a master table through which researchers can find an approximation of the required sample size, given a fixed set of statistical power and test significance levels prespecified by the researchers. In general, the smaller the proportion of the family type of interest or the higher its correlation with other predictor variables, the larger the sample size necessary for adequate power and the greater the probability the researchers will fail to reject the null hypothesis when the null is actually false (i.e., Type II error). Interestingly, because increases in the subsample size of atypical families tend to increase the probability of rejecting the null hypothesis (except in the case where an effect size is truly zero) (Kramer & Rosenthal, 1999), a significant finding from a small-subsample size also gives the researchers a greater leverage to claim that a disparity may actually exist.

ADDITIONAL APPROACHES

In this article, we discuss the potential problems and solutions when using extant data to compare small subsamples of atypical families with other family structures. When researchers have some control over data collection processes, however, the best solution for incomplete data or small-sample problems is to add or improve data through a carefully planned investigation (Hahn & Meeker, 1991). For example, experimental researchers often contend that one can maximize statistical power without an increase in sample size by improving the reliability of their outcome measures (Venter & Maxwell, 1999).

This said, many scholars are forced to answer their research questions using the best data, often from secondary data sets, that they already have in hand. In this situation, researchers may need to reorient their research so that their questions can be addressed by statistical methods appropriate for smaller subsamples. In addition to the potential solutions we have discussed above, researchers may consider using other analytical approaches. For example, propensity matching can be a useful method for comparing a small-sample group with a large alternative group in the population. By reducing the imbalance between the treatment and the control group in the distribution of covariates, this method can sometimes result in lower standard errors and thus increase the statistical power of analysis. (For the pros and cons of propensity matching, see Smith, 1997, and Morgan, 2001; also see Frisco, Muller, & Frank, in press.) When multiple indicators are available to measure the same theoretical construct, structural equation modeling can be used to develop latent variables that are freer of measurement errors (Hoyle & Kenny, 1999). This allows the researchers to lessen the problem of unreliability of their dependent variables and in turn increase the statistical power of their analysis. For both the latent variable approach and our earlier recommendation to use multiple dependent measures, the key lies in the search for a consistent pattern. This central idea is analogous to the person-centered approach (mostly used by psychologists), in which researchers focus on the overall consistency of an individual's profile of behaviors and person traits (Furr & Funder, 2004). Finally, others suggest that meta-analysis can be used to combine the effect sizes of previous studies using similar analytical strategies (Hershberger, Wallace, Green, & Marquis, 1999; Kramer & Rosenthal, 1999). To the extent that studies, regardless of their sample size, adequately measure the same underlying phenomenon, meta-analysis provides a viable alternative to overcome the small-subsample problem.

CONCLUSION

This paper explores a problem that family scholars frequently encounter when using secondary data to study atypical families. Even with a large nationally representative data set, the small number of atypical families often makes it difficult to be confident about conclusions based on these data. We contend that because small-subsample sizes are sensitive to sampling errors and have small statistical power to reject a null hypothesis, researchers must be extremely careful when identifying atypical families and generalizing their empirical findings. In particular, when researchers find non-significant results from their small-subsample analysis, additional checks are required to ensure that their conclusions would hold in analyses using larger subgroup samples.

Using the ECLS-K data, we demonstrate the problems of small-subsample sizes and propose potential solutions. By comparing influences of different subsample sizes on the academic achievements of children from Asian/White interracial and White monoracial families, we show that an increase of subsample size can increase significance levels of empirical findings and a reduction of subsample size may obscure possible (dis)advantages of atypical family forms. Through power analysis, we also demonstrate that the statistical power (or the minimum subsample size of atypical families to reach an acceptable level of statistical power) can be easily calculated.

The strategies proposed here, many of them originating from methodologists in other sub- fields, do not entirely solve the problem of small-subsample size. There are limits to what we can do with small numbers of atypical families in secondary data sets. As a result, every effort must be made to improve the quality and increase the quantity of our data. The bottom line is that more cases and more reliable measures always provide greater power to understand newly emergent nontraditional families.

Our exercise demonstrates a fundamental lesson from social science research: Reliance on small subsamples, even with a nationally representative data set, can yield questionable findings. For many readers, this insight is not new. Yet, all too frequently, the limitations resulting from small-sample sizes are minimized or completely overlooked in studies of atypical families. By no means should our study be construed as a blanket objection to the use of small subsamples in the analysis of atypical families. Indeed, we have relied on small subsamples in our own studies. Our point is that, when small subsamples are used, researchers should be circumspect regarding their findings. As vital as it is to study the experiences of nontraditional families, work in this area must meet the same rigorous standard that we expect of other family scholarship. To do otherwise is a disservice to our field, particularly in view of the likelihood that critics will use poorly done projects as "evidence" of political biases in academic research. Because robust findings typically can withstand the use of slightly different coding, subsample sizes, other dependent variables, and other controls, such prudent examinations can increase both our confidence in substantive conclusions and the credibility of our research community as a whole.

Title:
Suicidal ideation among racial/ethnic minorities: Moderating effects of rumination and depressive symptoms.
Authors:
Cheref, Soumia, Department of Psychology, Hunter College, City University of New York, New York, NY, US
Lane, Robert, Department of Psychology, Hunter College, City University of New York, New York, NY, US
Polanco-Roman, Lillian, Department of Psychology, Graduate Center, City University of New York, New York, NY, US
Gadol, Erin, Department of Psychology, Hunter College, City University of New York, New York, NY, US
Miranda, Regina, Department of Psychology, Hunter College, City University of New York, New York, NY, US, regina.miranda@hunter.cuny.edu
Address:
Miranda, Regina, Department of Psychology, Hunter College, City University of New York, 695 Park Avenue, Room 611HN, New York, NY, US, 10065, regina.miranda@hunter.cuny.edu
Source:
Cultural Diversity and Ethnic Minority Psychology, Vol 21(1), Jan, 2015. pp. 31-40.
Page Count:
10
Publisher:
US : Educational Publishing Foundation
Other Journal Titles:
Cultural Diversity and Mental Health
Other Publishers:
US : John Wiley & Sons, Inc.
ISSN:
1099-9809 (Print)
1939-0106 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
race, ethnicity, rumination, depression, suicidal ideation
Abstract:
Among emerging adults and college students, racial and ethnic minorities experience greater risk for suicidal ideation and behavior than their White counterparts. Research has identified numerous cognitive risk factors for suicidal ideation. However, they have not been well studied among racial and ethnic minorities. The present study examined the association between these factors (brooding rumination, reflective rumination, hopelessness, and depressive symptoms) and suicidal ideation among 690 Black, Latino, and biracial college students. Among all groups, hopelessness was positively associated with suicidal ideation. Brooding was negatively associated with suicidal ideation, after adjusting for reflection and hopelessness, although only at low levels of depressive symptoms. Black race/ethnicity and Latino race/ethnicity, compared with biracial race/ethnicity, each separately interacted with reflection to predict lower levels of suicidal ideation at moderate to high levels of reflection. Furthermore, Latino race/ethnicity, compared with biracial race/ethnicity, interacted with both reflection and depressive symptoms, such that reflection was negatively associated with suicidal ideation among Latino individuals reporting depressive symptoms above the 39th percentile. Biracial race/ethnicity, compared with monoracial race/ethnicity, also interacted with reflection and depressive symptoms, with reflection associated with greater amounts of suicidal ideation at depressive symptom levels above the 39th percentile. Our findings suggest reflective rumination differentially affects racial and ethnic groups and should be considered in conjunction with depressive symptoms among Latino and biracial individuals in suicide risk assessment and treatment. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2015 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
Testing models of life adjustment of adolescents from new immigrant families in Taiwan.
Authors:
Chen, Yu-Wen, Department of Social Work, National Taiwan University, Taipei, Taiwan
Source:
Bulletin of Educational Psychology, Vol 42(1), Sep, 2010. pp. 29-52.
Page Count:
24
Publisher:
Taiwan : National Taiwan Normal University

Language:

Keywords:
models, life adjustment, adolescents, immigrant families, Taiwan, mix heritage
Abstract:
This study attempted to compare life adjustment of adolescents from mix-heritage (one parent from Southeast Asian countries) and single-heritage families. It also investigated factors associated with problems in adjustment of adolescents from new immigrant families in their academic and social lives, including educational performance, peer relationship, psychological and behavioral problems from the theoretical perspectives of identity formation and symbolic interaction, factors associated with the of adolescents from new immigrant families. Using the multi-stage cluster sampling method, 884 adolescents aged 12 to 16 participated in this study. Three hundred and ninety-one were biracial (44.2%) and 493 were monoracial (55.8%). No difference was found between groups in all measures of life adjustment. Path analyses indicated that relationships between the proposed explanatory variables and life adjustment were not quite the same for the two groups of adolescents. However, minority racial identity and perceived racial discrimination predicted the life adjustment of adolescents from new immigrant families. Minority racial identity had both direct and indirect effects on psychological and behavioral adjustment, but had indirect effects on educational performance and peer relationship. Perceived racial discrimination only had indirect effects on life adjustment via self-esteem. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
The black-white-other achievement gap: Testing theories of academic performance among multiracial and monoracial adolescents.
Authors:
Herman, Melissa R., Department of Sociology, Dartmouth College, Hanover, NH, US, melissa.herman@dartmouth.edu
Address:
Herman, Melissa R., Department of Sociology, Dartmouth College, 6104 Silsby Hall, Hanover, NH, US, 03655, melissa.herman@dartmouth.edu
Source:
Sociology of Education, Vol 82(1), Jan, 2009. pp. 20-46.
Page Count:
27
Publisher:
US : American Sociological Assn
Other Journal Titles:
Journal of Educational Sociology
Other Publishers:
US : Sage Publications
ISSN:
0038-0407 (Print)
1939-8573 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
academic performance, multiracial adolescents, monoracial adolescents, achievement differences, high school students, racial differences
Abstract:
The study presented here tested three theories of racial differences in academic performance among monoracial and multiracial high school students. These theories (status attainment, oppositional culture, and educational attitudes) were developed to explain differences in achievement among monoracial groups, but the study tested how the theories apply to a multiracial sample. The results show that ethnic identity and experiences of ethnic discrimination are not strong factors in explaining academic performance among multiracial or monoracial students. Instead, the grades of multiracial students are related to their concrete beliefs about the consequences of school failure, the educational values of their peers, and the racial composition of their neighborhoods and schools. Additional descriptive statistics found that multiracial students who self-identify as black or Hispanic achieve lower grades than do those who self-identify as white or Asian. The author suggests a transracial theory of academic performance that considers the effects of contexts. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:
The effect of stereotypical primes on the neural processing of racially ambiguous faces.
Authors:
Dickter, Cheryl L., Department of Psychology, College of William and Mary, Williamsburg, VA, US, cldickter@wm.edu
Kittel, Julie A., Department of Psychology, College of William and Mary, Williamsburg, VA, US
Address:
Dickter, Cheryl L., Department of Psychology, College of William and Mary, PO Box 8795, Williamsburg, VA, US, 23187-8795, cldickter@wm.edu
Source:
Social Neuroscience, Vol 7(6), Nov, 2012. pp. 622-631.
Page Count:
10
Publisher:
United Kingdom : Taylor & Francis
ISSN:
1747-0919 (Print)
1747-0927 (Electronic)
Language:
English
Keywords:
stereotypical primes, neural processing, racially ambiguous faces, event related potential
Abstract:
Previous research has demonstrated that an early attentional component of the event-related potential (ERP), the P2, is sensitive to the distinction between the processing of racial outgroup and ingroup faces but may not be sensitive to the distinction between racially ambiguous and ingroup faces. Recent behavioral work, however, has suggested that contextual information may affect the processing of racially ambiguous faces. Thus, the first goal of this study was to examine whether the early neural processing of racially ambiguous faces would be affected by primed stereotypes. White college student participants (n = 29) completed a task in which they racially categorized monoracial Black and White faces and racially ambiguous Black–White morphs. These faces were preceded by positive and negative Black and White stereotypical primes. Results indicated that P2 amplitude to the racially ambiguous faces was moderated by the valence of the primes such that negative primes led to greater neural processing of the racially ambiguous faces than positive primes. Furthermore, the extent to which P2 amplitude was affected by prime valence was moderated by individual differences in preference for structure and categorical thinking, as well as comfort with ambiguity. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

'The hegemonic male and Kosoval nationalism, 2000-2005': Erratum.

Keywords:

Abstract:
Reports an error in "The hegemonic male and Kosovar nationalism, 2000-2005" by Jamie Munn (Men and Masculinities, 2008[Apr], Vol 10[4], 440-456). The original article included substantial portions of text from an article entitled "Masculinity and Nationalism: Gender and Sexuality in the Making of Nations, written by Dr. Joane Nagel and published in the March 2008 Issue of Ethnic and Racial Studies. The author did not provide attribution to Dr. Nagel for the portion of her article used. (The following abstract of the original article appeared in record 2008-06367-005). The article addresses the link between manhood and nationhood in postconflict Kosova. Albanian Kosovars, like many "traditionally" patriarchal societies, have constructed identities of the patriotic man and the exalted childbearing woman as icons of national survival. These designated identities often negate the realities of war-affected communities. The gendered places of man and woman in political reality are marred by the traumatic events of conflict and postconflict life. By thinking about the masculine microcultures of nation building (daily life), especially the construction of over-sexed and under-sexed individuals (i.e., the soldier) and the promiscuous enemies within (i.e., the female rape victim), there develops a connection between monoracial and heterosexual preserves and the need for this society to hold onto the traditional vision of man, at least until there is the political union of nation and state. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2012 APA, all rights reserved)

Language:
English
Keywords:

Abstract:
Using data from the National Longitudinal Study of Adolescent Health (Add Health), this study investigates patterns of well-being among multiracial adolescents. Specifically, this article addresses three questions. First, using various categorizations for multiracial background, are there measurable differences in emotional and social well-being among White, minority, and multiracial adolescents? Second, do multiracial adolescents with a White mother tend to fare differently than those with a minority mother? Third, does variation in family-based social capital—including parental involvement, parent-child relationship quality, and family structure—contribute to observed well-being differences among multiracial and monoracial adolescents? Results suggest that multiracial adolescents experience more negative social and emotional well-being outcomes when their mother is a minority. This finding persists even when controlling for sources of family-based social capital. (PsycINFO Database Record (c) 2015 APA, all rights reserved) (journal abstract)

Title:

LeCroy, Craig W., Arizona State University, Phoenix, AZ, US

Abstract:
This study examined predictors of substance use and negative activity involvement among a diverse sample of European American, African American, Hispanic, Native American, and multiracial early adolescents (n = 749) living in a large urban city in the Southwest United States. This study investigated a broad set of predictor variables that tap sociodemographic, peer, family, community, and school influences. Overall, findings from this study confirm that lifetime substance use remains high among youth of color. Of particular concern is this study's finding that multiracial adolescents are at elevated risk to use substances and engage in negative activities. The implications of this study for understanding how risk factors are influenced by race and other variables on different measures of problem behavior are discussed.
